- France and Germany: English ads underperform compared to local-language ads - create localized content
- Nordic countries, Netherlands, Middle East: English ads perform fine - no localization needed
- If grouping an expensive/crowded market (US, UK) with smaller markets, most budget goes to the crowded market, starving the others. Keep expensive markets separate.

### Splitting by Company Size

| Segment | Employee Count | Characteristics |
|---------|---------------|-----------------|
| SMB | 0-200 employees | Faster sales cycles, lower ACV, cost-sensitive |
| Mid-Market | 200-500 employees | Moderate sales cycles, balanced ACV |
| Enterprise | 500+ employees | Longer sales cycles, higher ACV, multi-stakeholder |

**Rules:**
- Use employee count, not revenue - LinkedIn has no access to private company revenue data, only estimates. Employee count is accurate.
- Start with 2 splits, not 3 (e.g., SMB + Enterprise combined, or SMB vs Mid-Market/Enterprise)
- The 500-employee threshold is the standard enterprise cutoff - buying processes get significantly slower above this
- Also split by industry when product has significantly different use cases per vertical
- **LinkedIn distribution bias:** If you target all company sizes together, LinkedIn will show your ads mostly to companies with 50-200 employees and 10,000+ employees (most LinkedIn users work at these sizes). Mid-market companies (200-5,000) get underserved. Splitting by size forces fair distribution.

### Splitting by Seniority / Audience Role

Split into decision-makers vs individual contributors when product is used by one group but purchased/approved by another.

**Why:** Decision-makers get ROI-focused ads. Individual contributors get daily-task-focused ads showing how the product helps their work. Contributors influence the purchase decision (sometimes called "dark social").

**Three ways to split:**

1. **Decision-makers vs Individual contributors** (most common)
   - Campaign 1: Directors, VPs, C-levels -> ROI/strategic messaging
   - Campaign 2: Managers, specialists, executives -> Productivity/daily-use messaging

2. **By job title groups**
   - Campaign 1: Marketing managers, specialists
   - Campaign 2: Marketing directors, VP Marketing, CMO

3. **By job function + seniority levels**
   - Campaign 1: Marketing function + entry, senior, manager seniorities
   - Campaign 2: Marketing function + director, VP, C-level seniorities

**Note:** Splitting by years of experience is rare and only useful when product use case depends on experience. LinkedIn counts total experience including unrelated jobs - "5+ years" may include unrelated prior roles. Splitting by member skills is very rare (pharmaceutical/highly technical products only).

**When to split by seniority:** Only after campaigns are already split by intent, persona, region, and company size. Each split requires unique messaging, creatives, and landing pages.

**Track influence, not just conversions:** One audience may not convert directly but influences the purchase. Use attribution tools to track the LinkedIn impression journey and tie impressions to pipeline even when they don't directly convert. Example: A CMO sees the ad, likes it, sends it to their team, and the team takes action - the CMO's campaign appears to have zero conversions but actually drove the deal. Set up self-reported attribution ("how did you find us?") alongside impression-based attribution to capture dark social influence.

## Audience Expansion Models

Three types of audience expansion on LinkedIn. Use as a **last resort** - try job functions, different industries, different regions first.

| Model | Source | How Created | Setup Time | Audience Size | When to Use |
|-------|--------|-------------|-----------|--------------|-------------|
| Audience Expansion | Professional demographics | LinkedIn algorithm finds similar profiles | Auto-enabled (turn OFF) | Depends on campaign + budget | Using specific facets where you might miss members |
| Lookalike | Any matched audience list | 1st-party data + LinkedIn algorithm | 48-72 hours | 5x-10x of source audience | Expanding account lists or retargeting lists |
| Predictive Audience | Contact list, conversions, or lead gen forms | Predictive AI + your data | 4-5 days | Adjustable slider, max 10% of location population | Controlling size by geo-location with conversion data |

**Critical:** Audience Expansion is auto-enabled in campaign setup. Always turn it OFF. LinkedIn's similarity matching is unreliable - e.g., targeting marketing managers -> LinkedIn finds Harvard attendance pattern -> targets Harvard alumni regardless of role.

**Match rates across all three models are generally low and unqualified.** Test extensively before committing meaningful budget.

## Cross-Channel Retargeting: Paid Search + LinkedIn

For companies spending $30K+/month on paid search (Google Ads, Bing Ads), create a cross-channel retargeting strategy:

**How it works:**
1. Tag all paid search traffic with UTM parameters (e.g., `utm_source=google`, `utm_medium=cpc`, or `utm_source=paid_search`)
2. Create a custom LinkedIn remarketing audience filtering for website visitors with those specific UTM parameters
3. Run LinkedIn retargeting campaigns specifically to people who came from paid search

**Why this is powerful:** Paid search visitors have demonstrated active buying intent (they searched for your solution). Retargeting them on LinkedIn with brand-building and social proof content reinforces the brand during their evaluation process. This combines intent signals from search with LinkedIn's professional targeting.

**Minimum threshold:** Only worth the effort if paid search drives $30K+/month in spend and generates meaningful traffic volume. Below that, the retargeting audience will be too small to serve ads to.

## LinkedIn Sales Navigator as Audience Extension

LinkedIn Sales Navigator provides a complementary organic outreach channel alongside paid ads.

**Profile visitor connection strategy:** When someone views your LinkedIn profile (often triggered by seeing your ads or TLAs), send them a blank connection request through Sales Navigator. Acceptance rate on these is approximately 60% - significantly higher than cold connection requests - because they've already shown interest by visiting your profile.

**How to operationalize:**
- Check profile visitors daily in Sales Navigator
- Send connection requests to visitors who match your ICP
- Do not include a sales pitch in the connection request (blank performs best)
- Once connected, nurture through organic content in their feed
- After a few touchpoints, initiate a conversation naturally

This creates a free, organic layer on top of your paid ABM and awareness campaigns.

## Audience Exclusion Layers

Apply these in order of priority:

1. **Essential:** Converted users excluded from ALL campaigns
2. **Essential:** Competitors and existing customers excluded from cold
3. **Essential:** Website visitors excluded from prospecting (they go to retargeting)
4. **Preferred:** Unpaid/entry-level seniority excluded
5. **Preferred:** Agency and business employees excluded
6. **Optional:** Lookalike audiences not mixed with job title/skill audiences in same ad set
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/audit-checklist.md

```
# LinkedIn Ads Account Audit Checklist

Use this when onboarding a new account, doing periodic health checks, or diagnosing underperformance.

## How to Use

Go through each item. Mark as Pass/Fail/NA. For any Fail, document the action needed and expected impact. Priority: Essential items first, then Preferred, then Optional.

## 1. Tracking & Attribution

| # | Check | Description | Priority |
|---|-------|-------------|----------|
| 1.1 | LinkedIn Insight Tag installed and firing | Verifies accurate visitor and conversion tracking | Essential |
| 1.2 | Primary conversion events firing on actual completion | Signup, demo, free trial - must fire on success, not button click | Essential |
| 1.3 | Secondary conversion events set up | Contact form, asset download - supplementary tracking | Preferred |
| 1.4 | Conversions trigger on successful completion (not button click) | Ensures conversions = real actions, not just intent | Essential |
| 1.5 | Correct conversion event selected at campaign level | Each campaign aligned with appropriate conversion action | Essential |
| 1.6 | UTM parameters on all ad URLs | Enables tracking in analytics tools (GA4, etc.) | Essential |

## 2. Campaign Setup

| # | Check | Description | Priority |
|---|-------|-------------|----------|
| 2.1 | Campaigns segmented into prospecting vs retargeting | Different strategies, messaging, budgets for new vs returning | Essential |
| 2.2 | Clear naming conventions | Easy identification and management | Essential |
| 2.3 | Retargeting using lead or conversion objectives | Moves retargeted users down the funnel | Preferred |
| 2.4 | Each campaign gets 10+ results/week (ideally 30) | Algorithm needs enough data to optimize | Preferred |
| 2.5 | Daily budgets sufficient for 10 results/week | Adequate funding for positive results | Preferred |

## 3. Targeting

| # | Check | Description | Priority |
|---|-------|-------------|----------|
| 3.1 | Audience Expansion de-selected | Avoids overly broad targeting | Preferred |
| 3.2 | LinkedIn Audience Network de-selected | Avoids irrelevant placements | Essential |
| 3.3 | Not using age, gender, or language targeting | LinkedIn has limited data on these - restricts delivery | Essential |
| 3.4 | Audiences narrowed using AND (not OR) | More specific targeting | Preferred |
| 3.5 | Campaigns grouped by similar countries | Better budget control - CPAs vary significantly by region | Preferred |
| 3.6 | Lookalike audiences tested | Expands reach based on successful audiences | Preferred |
| 3.7 | Prospecting audiences have 100K+ users | Wide enough reach while maintaining specificity | Preferred |
| 3.8 | Website visitors excluded from prospecting campaigns | Avoids wasted spend - visitors go to retargeting | Essential |
| 3.9 | Converted audiences excluded from ALL campaigns | Avoids re-targeting people who already converted | Essential |
| 3.10 | Lookalikes not mixed with job title/skill audiences in same ad set | Prevents overlap and dilution | Essential |
| 3.11 | Campaign naming matches actual targeting in ad sets | Clarity between names and settings | Essential |
| 3.12 | Unpaid and entry-level seniority excluded | Focus spend on qualified audiences (only applies when using seniority-based targeting, not job title targeting - these are mutually exclusive) | Optional |
| 3.13 | Agency and business employees excluded | Focus spend on qualified audiences | Preferred |

## 4. Creatives & Ads

| # | Check | Description | Priority |
|---|-------|-------------|----------|
| 4.1 | Correct forms selected for on-platform lead campaigns | Accurate data collection | Essential |
| 4.2 | Copy and creatives match each funnel stage | Aligned messaging throughout journey | Preferred |
| 4.3 | Landing pages relevant to ad copies and creatives | Cohesive user experience | Preferred |
| 4.4 | CTA on ad matches CTA on landing page | Consistent message | Preferred |
| 4.5 | Ad creatives high quality, images not blurred | Professional presentation | Preferred |
| 4.6 | No spelling or grammar errors in ad copy or creative | Professionalism and clarity | Preferred |
| 4.7 | At least 4 active ads per campaign | Enables testing and optimization | Preferred |
| 4.8 | Multiple ad formats being used (video, document, image, carousel) | Enhanced engagement | Preferred |
| 4.9 | Video ads under 2 minutes with subtitles | Clarity and accessibility | Preferred |
| 4.10 | Ad copies have clear CTA | Drives engagement and action | Essential |
| 4.11 | Ad sizes suited for platform (no cropping) | Ads appear as intended | Essential |
| 4.12 | Ads launched less than 3 months ago | Content fresh, ad fatigue minimized | Preferred |

## Audit Scoring

- Count Essential items: Pass/Total
- Count Preferred items: Pass/Total
- Count Optional items: Pass/Total
- Overall health: All Essential passing = healthy foundation. Any Essential failing = critical fix needed.

## Common Audit Findings (ranked by frequency)

1. Audience Expansion left ON (most common mistake)
2. No retargeting audiences set up
3. Conversion tracking firing on button click instead of completion
4. UTM parameters missing
5. Too few ads per campaign (1-2 instead of 4+)
6. Naming conventions inconsistent
7. No exclusions (competitors, customers, entry-level)
8. Budget too spread across too many campaigns
9. Creative not refreshed in 3+ months
10. LinkedIn Audience Network left ON
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/benchmarks.md

```
# B2B SaaS LinkedIn Ads Benchmarks

## CTR Benchmarks by Funnel Stage (200+ B2B SaaS accounts)

**IMPORTANT: CTR terminology varies by campaign objective.** For Engagement objective campaigns (including TLAs), LinkedIn's "CTR" metric is actually **engagement rate** (reactions, comments, post clicks) not click-through-rate to landing page. **CTRTLP** (CTR to landing page) is the real CTR to off-platform destinations. The benchmarks below (0.30%-1.30%) apply to **CTRTLP**, not to the "CTR" column reported for Engagement campaigns.

| Audience Type | CTRTLP Range | Notes |
|--------------|-----------|-------|
| Cold (TOF) | 0.30% - 0.55% | Anything above 0.44% is above platform average. For Engagement campaigns, compare engagement rate, not post CTR |
| Warm / Retargeting (MOF) | 0.55% - 0.80% | Higher because audience already knows you |
| Engaged / Converted (BOF) | 0.80% - 1.30% | Highest intent, highest CTR to landing page |

**Reality check:** 0.45% is the overall LinkedIn Ads average CTRTLP. Anything above 1% is genuinely good. Above 1.5% for Sponsored Content is strong. Message Ads should target >1.5% CTRTLP.

## CPM and CPC Benchmarks

| Metric | Range |
|--------|-------|
| CPM (avg) | $33 - $65 |
| CPC (standard brand ads) | $8 - $22+ |
| CPC (TLAs) | $4.14 (can go as low as) |
| CPM (Spotlight Ads) | ~$5.23 (74% lower than single image) |
| Conversation Ads CTR | 1% - 5% (CTA clicks / sends) |

## Cost Per Conversion / Lead Benchmarks

| Metric | Range | Notes |
|--------|-------|-------|
| Cost per engagement | $2 - $5 | Below $3 is strong |
| Cost per conversion (on-platform) | $15 - $50 | Varies heavily by industry and offer |
| Cost per lead (Lead Gen Form) | $50 - $200 | Depends on offer value and audience |
| Cost per form submission (website) | $200 - $500 | Higher friction than native forms |
| SQL cost | Should not exceed $500 | Varies by ACV |

## Form Fill Rate

- Target: above 8%
- Below 8% = form is too long, offer isn't compelling enough, or audience isn't warm enough

## TLA-Specific Benchmarks

| Metric | TLA Performance | Standard Ad Performance |
|--------|----------------|------------------------|
| CTR | 1.7x higher | Baseline |
| Engagement rate | 1.6x higher | Baseline |
| CPC | As low as $4.14 | $8-$22+ |
| Company page ads comparison | 3-6x higher CTR | Baseline |

**Why TLAs win:** Ads from real executives feel organic in the feed. They build trust. They don't look like ads. LinkedIn's algorithm favors them because they drive genuine engagement.

## Ad Format Performance Ranking

Based on cross-source data:

| Rank | Format | Best For | Cost Efficiency |
|------|--------|----------|----------------|
| 1 | Thought Leader Ads (TLA) | TOF engagement + BOF conversion | Highest - lowest CPC, highest CTR |
| 2 | Video TLAs (VTLA) | TOF awareness + retargeting pool | High - strong conversion rates |
| 3 | Video Ads (10-30s) | TOF awareness | Good for retargeting pool building |
| 4 | Document Ads | MOF nurture | High engagement, good for retargeting |
| 5 | Single Image | All stages | Versatile, moderate performance |
| 6 | Carousel | MOF/BOF | Good for feature showcases |
| 7 | Spotlight Ads | MOF reinforcement | Very cheap CPM (~$5.23) |
| 8 | Text Ads | MOF brand reinforcement | Cheapest, always-on |
| 9 | Lead Gen Forms | BOF conversion | Lower friction, higher completion |
| 10 | Conversation Ads | MOF/BOF | 1-5% CTR, new retargeting options |
| 11 | CTV/Brand Awareness | Not recommended at < $15K/mo | Too expensive for small budgets |

## ROAS Benchmarks

- B2B SaaS average ROAS on LinkedIn: ~113%
- This is a lagging metric - LinkedIn Ads impact is often seen 3-6 months after engagement
- Pipeline attribution is more meaningful than last-click ROAS for LinkedIn

## Key Ratios to Monitor

| Ratio | What It Tells You |
|-------|-------------------|
| Engagement rate > 1% | Creative resonates |
| Landing page CTR / Total CTR | How many engagers actually visit your site (often shockingly low) |
| Video view rate (starts/impr) > 40% | Creative hooks attention (horizontal 16:9) |
| Video completion rate (completions/starts) 8-15% | Content holds attention (horizontal 16:9). Sub-3% for vertical |
| Cost per 50% view < $4 | Video cost efficiency. $2-4 avg, $0.21 best case |
| Retargeting pool growth rate | TOF is doing its job |
| BOF conversion rate > 5% | Retargeting audience is warm enough |

## Industry Context

These benchmarks are for B2B SaaS. Adjust expectations for:
- **Enterprise / high ACV:** Higher CPLs are acceptable ($300-$500+) because deal sizes justify it
- **SMB / low ACV:** Need cheaper CPLs ($50-$150) to make unit economics work
- **Healthcare vertical:** Often higher CPMs due to smaller, specialized audiences
- **Tech vertical:** More competitive, benchmark CPCs tend to be at the higher end
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/bidding-strategy.md

```
# Bidding Strategy & Weekly Optimization

## Bidding Approach

### Phase 1: Automated (Week 1 of any new campaign)

Start every new campaign with automated/maximum delivery bidding. This lets LinkedIn's algorithm learn who engages and at what cost. Don't touch bids during this phase.

### Phase 2: Manual (Week 2+)

After week 1, switch to manual CPC bidding at **20% below your average CPC** from the automated phase. This works ~99% of the time for significant CPC reductions.

### Exceptions

- **Small retargeting/ABM audiences:** Keep automated bidding - manual bidding on tiny audiences causes underdelivery
- **New campaign objectives:** Reset to automated for the first week when changing objectives
- **Undersized audiences (<10K):** May not spend full budgets regardless of bid amounts

### Bid Strategy by Objective

| Objective | Recommended Bid Type | Notes |
|-----------|---------------------|-------|
| Engagement (TOF) | Start automated, switch manual | Optimize for lowest cost per engagement |
| Video Views (TOF) | Automated usually fine | Video views are typically cheap on LinkedIn |
| Website Visits (MOF/BOF) | Start automated, switch manual | Monitor CPC closely |
| Lead Gen Forms (BOF) | Cost cap or manual | Set cap at target CPL |
| Conversions (BOF) | Automated for small audiences, manual for large | Depends on audience size |

## Weekly Optimization Checklist

Most advertisers underestimate the power of weekly demographic audits. Do this every week:

### Step 1: Check Demographics Report

Open Campaign Manager, Demographics tab for each active campaign. Review:
- **Job Title:** Are irrelevant titles consuming budget? Exclude them.
- **Job Function:** Aligned with ICP?
- **Seniority:** Any unpaid/training/entry slipping through? Exclude.
- **Industry:** Are off-target industries showing up? Exclude the ones with impressions but no engagement.
- **Company Size:** Aligned with target?
- **Company Name:** Any competitors showing up? Exclude.

### Step 2: Review Performance Metrics

| Metric | Check | Action if failing |
|--------|-------|-------------------|
| CTR (TOF) | Above 0.44% avg? | Refresh creative or widen audience |
| CTR (BOF) | Above 3-5%? | Strengthen CTA or tighten retargeting |
| CPE | Below $5? | Good. Above $5 = review targeting |
| Frequency | Below 3/week per person? | Above 3 = rotate creative or expand audience |
| Audience penetration | Above 10% in first 2 weeks? | Below 10% = audience too large or budget too low |
| Spend pacing | Spending full daily budget? | Underspending = audience too small or bid too low |

### Step 3: Creative Health

- Any ad running for 3+ months? Refresh it
- Are there 4+ active ads per campaign? If not, add more
- Check for creative fatigue: declining CTR over 2+ weeks, rotate
- **Ad rotation scheduling:** New ads should start with even rotation (equal budget per ad). After 7-10 days of data, switch to auto rotation so the algorithm favors winners. This prevents premature "winner" selection before enough data is collected.

### Step 4: Budget Reallocation

- Shift budget from underperforming campaigns to outperformers
- Don't make more than 20% budget changes at once (algorithm needs stability)
- If a campaign has less than 10 results/week, it lacks enough data to optimize

### Step 5: Check Audience Penetration

Review audience penetration data to determine if budgets need adjusting:

| Timeframe | Penetration Threshold | Action |
|-----------|----------------------|--------|
| 30 days | Below 25% of audience | Increase budget |
| 60 days | Below 40% of audience | Increase budget |
| Target | 35%+ penetration | Healthy - maintain |

**Key insight:** Doubling budget typically boosts penetration by 50-70%, not 100%. One well-budgeted campaign at 35%+ penetration outperforms three underfunded campaigns at 12% each.

## Campaign Group Budget Optimization

LinkedIn's group budget feature lets the algorithm allocate budget across campaigns within a group instead of setting individual campaign budgets.

### Test Results ($25M+ Data)

| Metric | Group Budget | Campaign Budget |
|--------|-------------|----------------|
| Cost per reach | ~10% cheaper | Baseline |
| Audience penetration | Nearly 2x higher | Baseline |
| In-platform metrics (CTR, engagement) | Marginally better (~1%) | Baseline |

**Key finding:** One month's audience penetration with group budget = two months' audience penetration without. The algorithm is better at distributing spend for maximum reach.

### Caveats

- **One objective per group** - can't mix reach + traffic objectives
- **Algorithm favors cheaper audiences** - non-enterprise campaigns get the lion's share of budget in mixed groups
- **Video bias** - if mixing static + video in one group, 80%+ budget may go to video
- **Recommendation:** Be as granular as possible. Don't mix enterprise + SMB campaigns, or static + video campaigns, in the same group

### Budget Approach for New Campaign Types

- **Given extra budget:** Start new campaign at half the original campaign budget, increase in 2 weeks if performance is good ("smoke testing")
- **No extra budget:** Use campaign group budget option, but expect LinkedIn to prioritize the cheapest campaign type

## Ad Scheduling and Rotation Tactics

### LinkedIn Ad Scheduling

LinkedIn's ad day starts at UTC midnight, which is 8:00 PM Eastern. This matters for budget pacing and scheduling.

**Recommended schedule:** Run ads from 5:00 AM to 2:00 PM Eastern (business hours when professionals are most active on LinkedIn). Pause during afternoons and evenings when engagement drops and CPMs remain high.

**Why this works:** LinkedIn's algorithm resets daily budget at UTC midnight. By concentrating spend during peak engagement hours, you get more efficient delivery than spreading budget across 24 hours where much of it is wasted on low-engagement times.

### Ad Rotation Trick for Budget-Limited Accounts

If budget is tight and you need to run 6 campaigns but can only afford $30/day each:

- **Set A (3 campaigns):** Run Monday, Wednesday, Friday
- **Set B (3 campaigns):** Run Tuesday, Thursday, Saturday

This gives each campaign a $30/day budget on its active days, costing $30/day total instead of $60/day to run all six simultaneously. The campaigns still build reach over time, just at a 3-day-per-week pace instead of 7.

**When to use:** Budget-constrained accounts that need to test multiple audiences or creative approaches without doubling their daily spend.

## Sales Alignment and Lead Feedback

### Monthly Sales Alignment Meeting

Hold a monthly meeting between the ads team and sales to review:
- Which leads from LinkedIn are converting into pipeline
- Lead quality feedback by campaign and creative
- Which personas and messages are generating the best conversations
- Upcoming product launches or events to coordinate with ad campaigns

### Automated Lead Alerts via Slack

Set up real-time lead notifications using Zapier (or similar automation):
- When a new lead comes in from LinkedIn Lead Gen Forms, send to dedicated Slack channel
- Include key fields: company name, job title, form responses
- Sales team gives thumbs up (good lead) or thumbs down (bad lead) emoji reactions
- Track thumbs up/down ratio per campaign to measure lead quality over time

This creates a continuous feedback loop between paid ads and sales without requiring manual reporting.

## Monthly Review

- Review full-month ROAS and conversion data
- Assess strategic shifts needed
- Check retargeting pool growth (is TOF feeding the funnel?)
- Evaluate if audience segments need expanding or contracting
- Plan next month's creative rotation

## Quarterly Review

- Full performance audit (use audit-checklist.md)
- Strategy evaluation: is the funnel structure still right?
- Benchmark comparison: are we above/below industry?
- Budget planning for next quarter
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/campaign-structures.md

```
# Campaign Group Structures & Naming Conventions

## 6 Campaign Group Structure Models

Choose the right structure based on account complexity. For new accounts, start with structures 1-4. Scale to Pick and Mix as the account grows.

### 1. Objective-Based Structure

Organize groups by campaign objective. Makes it easy to measure performance against goals.

| Group | Objective | Example Campaigns |
|-------|-----------|-------------------|
| Awareness | Brand Awareness, Video Views | Brand video campaigns, thought leadership |
| Engagement | Engagement, Website Visits | Content/blog promotion, resource downloads |
| Conversion | Lead Gen, Website Conversions | Demo requests, free trial signups |

**Best for:** Simple accounts with one product/audience, small budgets.

### 2. Audience-Based Structure

Segment groups by target audience. Best when you have diverse markets with different messaging needs.

| Segment Type | Example Groups |
|-------------|----------------|
| Demographic | VP+ Engineering, Director+ Marketing, C-Suite |
| Geographic | US - Marketing, UK - Product, DACH - Engineering |
| Interest-Based | AI/ML Practitioners, DevOps Community |

**Best for:** Companies targeting multiple distinct personas or regions with tailored messaging.

### 3. Funnel Stage Structure

Align groups with the buyer's journey. This is the approach most recommended across all sources.

| Group | Stage | Content Type |
|-------|-------|-------------|
| TOF - Problem Aware | Prospecting cold audiences | Educational content, pain-point validation |
| MOF - Solution Aware | Retargeting engaged users | Case studies, comparisons, demos |
| BOF - Product Aware | Retargeting high-intent | Direct CTA, social proof, Lead Gen Forms |

**Best for:** Most B2B SaaS accounts. This is the default recommendation.

### 4. Product or Service Line Structure

Organize around each product/service for focused messaging and better tracking.

| Segment Type | Example Groups |
|-------------|----------------|
| By Product/Service | Voice AI Platform, Contact Center Analytics |
| By Use Case | Outbound Calling, Inbound Support, Appointment Setting |

**Best for:** Multi-product companies or companies with distinct use cases.

### 5. Testing Structure

Dedicated groups for controlled experiments. Essential for optimizing performance over time.

| Test Type | What You're Testing |
|-----------|-------------------|
| A/B Creative Tests | Different creatives or messaging within the same audience |
| Audience Tests | Comparing performance across different audience segments |
| Offer Tests | Trialling different offers or CTAs to see what resonates |

**Best for:** Mature accounts with enough budget to allocate to experimentation.

### 6. Pick and Mix Structure (Advanced)

For large accounts at scale, combine elements from multiple structures into one unified approach.

**Group naming data points:** Region, Persona, Seniority, Company Size, Product Line, Objective

**Example:** `US | VP+ Engineering | Enterprise | Voice AI | TOF`

Each group contains multiple campaigns. This is the only way to manage campaigns at scale without losing control.

**Best for:** Accounts spending $10K+/month with multiple products, regions, and personas. For starters, structures 1-4 are enough.

## Naming Convention System

Consistent naming is critical for reporting, filtering, and management at scale.

### Campaign Group Naming

Two conventions in use. Choose based on how you're structuring:

**Convention A - Stage + Awareness (5-Stage Demand Engine template):**
Format: `[Region] - [Stage] - [Awareness] - [Type] - {Detail}`

Examples:
- `NA - Capture - Solution Aware - Prospecting - {Persona}`
- `NA - Capture - Product Aware - Remarketing (Offers)`
- `NA - Activate - Offer Aware - Remarketing (Free Users)`
- `NA - Revive - Offer Aware - Remarketing (Closed Lost)`

**Convention B - Product/Persona focused (simpler):**
Format: `[Region] | [Product/Persona] | [Funnel Stage]`

Examples:
- `US | Voice AI | TOF - Prospecting`
- `UK | Contact Center | MOF - Retargeting`
- `Global | Brand | BOF - Conversion`

Convention A is better for accounts using the 5-Stage Demand Engine model. Convention B is simpler for basic 3-layer funnels.

### Campaign Naming

Format: `[Audience Type] - [Targeting Detail] - [Objective]`

Examples:
- `Cold - VP+ Engineering - Engagement`
- `Retarget - Website Visitors 30d - Lead Gen`
- `Lookalike - Converted Users - Website Conversions`

### Ad Naming

Format: `[Persona]_[Format]_[Creative#]_[CopyVariant]_{Date}`

Examples:
- `SalesDirector_Image1_Text1_2024-01`
- `VP_Engineering_Video2_TextA_2024-02`
- `CTO_Carousel1_TextB_2024-03`

This naming system enables:
- Quick filtering by any dimension in Campaign Manager
- Clean data exports for reporting tools (Looker Studio, etc.)
- Easy identification of what's running and what needs rotation

## 7. The Five Campaign Groups (Advanced Scaling)

At scale, organize campaigns into five purpose-driven groups. This replaces the simple awareness/retargeting split when you have enough budget and campaigns.

| Group | Purpose | Content Type | Priority Order |
|-------|---------|-------------|----------------|
| Product Value | Showcase what your product does and why it matters | Product demos, feature highlights, use cases | 1st (start here) |
| Remarketing | Re-engage people who already interacted | Demo offers, case studies, testimonials | 2nd |
| Content | Drive traffic with valuable knowledge | Reports, guides, blog posts, educational content | 3rd |
| Social Proof | Build trust through third-party validation | Customer testimonials, case studies, awards, reviews | 4th |
| Thought Leadership | Build brand affinity through expertise | Founder insights, industry analysis, opinion pieces | 5th (add last) |

**Funnel mapping:**
- Awareness stage -> Thought Leadership + Product Value groups
- Consideration stage -> Content + Social Proof groups
- Conversion stage -> Remarketing group

**Best for:** Accounts with 5+ campaigns and $5K+/month budget. Start with Product Value + Remarketing, add the others as budget grows.

## Splitting Campaigns by Type

Different campaign types (static, video, document, carousel, conversation, message ads) have different auctions on LinkedIn. Diversifying ad types is an auction strategy, not just a creative one.

**Key insight:** Static ads have the most competition. Document ads targeting the same audience will have less auction competition, leading to cheaper cost per reach.

**When to introduce a new campaign type:**
- Current campaign meeting/exceeding KPIs (audience is receptive, test more)
- Current campaign underperforming with no obvious issues (audience not receptive to format, try different)

**Campaign group budget option:** LinkedIn's campaign group budget lets the algorithm split budget between campaigns in a group. Caveat: LinkedIn favors video ads - you may see 80%+ of budget go to video when mixing static + video. For full budget control, use separate campaigns. See scaling-strategy.md for detailed findings.

## Scaling the Group Structure

As you scale, group naming should evolve in layers:

**Level 1 - Persona + Intent:**
- `Marketing - Awareness`
- `Marketing - Remarketing`
- `Sales - Awareness`
- `Sales - Remarketing`

**Level 2 - Add campaign group type:**
- `Sales - Awareness - Product Value`
- `Sales - Awareness - Content`
- `Sales - Remarketing - Demo`
- `Sales - Remarketing - Case Studies`

**Level 3 - Add region:**
- `Sales - Awareness - EMEA - Content`

**Level 4 - Add company size:**
- `Sales - Awareness - Product Value - Enterprise`

**Level 5 - Add audience segment:**
- `Sales - Awareness - Product Value - Enterprise - Decision Makers`

Under each group: individual campaigns with different creative types (static, video, document campaigns).

**When to split groups:** Start right away. Even with only 4 campaigns, put them in separate groups by persona and intent. Waiting creates a mess where group-level metrics mask individual performance. One strong retargeting campaign can make the entire mixed group look healthy while awareness campaigns silently fail.

**Moving campaigns between groups:** LinkedIn doesn't allow it. You must duplicate the campaign (losing historical data). Short-term pain, long-term clarity.

## Creative Rotation Configuration

When launching new ads within a campaign:
1. **Start with even rotation** - so each ad gets equal budget
2. **After 7-10 days** - check the data to see which ads perform best
3. **Then switch to auto rotation** - let the algorithm optimize delivery toward winners

This prevents the algorithm from prematurely picking a "winner" before enough data is collected.
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/conversation-ads.md

```
# Conversation Ads - Best Practices

## Format & Specs

- Subject line: max 60 characters. This is the ONLY thing prospects see before opening - if it doesn't grab attention, nothing else matters.
- Message body: up to 8,000 characters.
- Up to 5 CTA buttons (1 is the mandatory "Not Interested" - so 4 custom CTAs max).
- Branching decision tree supported (follow-up messages based on which CTA is clicked).
- Optional companion banner: 300x250 px (desktop only), JPG/PNG, max 2 MB.
- Can attach a Lead Gen Form (one per conversation).

## When to Use Conversation Ads vs Message Ads

**Conversation Ads** (choose-your-own-adventure, multiple CTA paths):
- Complex B2B buying journeys with multiple stakeholders
- Buyers who want to explore before committing
- ABM or high-intent campaigns
- Goal is to educate, qualify, and learn - not push immediate action
- When you want intent signals (every click tells you what the buyer cares about)

**Message Ads** (single message, single CTA):
- One clear goal (book a demo, download a guide)
- Quick, direct, cost-effective outreach
- Audience prefers short, no-nonsense messaging
- When you need simple, single-action conversions or low CPL

**Key distinction:** Message Ads talk *to* people. Conversation Ads speak *with* them. Conversation Ads are not built for instant wins - they influence interest over time.

## Copy Principles

1. **Write like a text, not a landing page.** Conversation ads go to an inbox. Short, casual, human. If it feels like a sales pitch, it's wrong.
2. **Keep messages to 3-5 short lines.** One idea per line. Product features, bullet lists, and detailed specs belong on the landing page or in the lead magnet - NOT in the message. One of the best-performing ads ever was "Hey, want to chat?"
3. **No more than 3 lines before a paragraph break.** Big blocks of text = reader scans and opts out. Force slow reading with frequent breaks.
4. **One sentence per line.** Makes the message scannable and easy to consume.
5. **Subject line: 2-4 words.** It's an inbox message - short subjects feel real, long ones scream ad. The first 50 characters of the opening message text also matter as preview.
6. **Be specific, not vague.** Use concrete numbers, timeframes, or outcomes. "29 mins and $105 gift card" > "a quick chat." Shock timing works - specific numbers force consumption.
7. **Emojis work in B2B (but match the brand).** Use directionally (pointing to CTA) and psychologically (associating words with outcomes). Skip emojis entirely if the brand voice doesn't use them.
8. **Don't be overly conversational either.** Be clear about the offer and the value. Tone can match the brand, but clarity > cleverness.
9. **Avoid buzzwords.** Words like "AI-powered," "optimize," "streamline," "game-changer" - buyers mentally skip them. Use specific problems, concrete outcomes, relatable frustrations. Specific beats impressive, every time.
10. **No feature dumps in the message.** The message's job is to earn the click. The details live behind the CTA buttons - on the demo page, in the lead magnet, or in a branch message. Don't try to sell in the inbox.

## CTA Strategy

1. **Clear CTA with a button.** Don't bury the action in text. Bad: "Would love to chat if you're interested." Good: "Book a 15-min demo" or "See the case study."
2. **Skip Action > Call to Action.** A Skip Action skips a step in the sales process (e.g., direct scheduling link instead of "fill out a form and we'll follow up"). Drastically improves pipeline efficiency by increasing form-to-demo attendance rate.
3. **Don't pitch a 1:1 upfront if the goal is registration/download.** Match the CTA to the actual goal - don't over-ask.
4. **Best-performing first CTAs are gentle, not aggressive.** "See how it works," "Find out more," "Get started for free" outperform "Book a Demo" as first step. Early-funnel CTAs reduce pressure, invite curiosity instead of commitment, feel helpful not transactional. Think of CTAs as doors, not demands.
5. **Offer multiple response options, not dead ends.** Let the buyer choose their path - content, FAQ, demo, pricing, "just browsing." Every click reveals intent.

## Psychology Triggers

Best conversation ads tap into basic human psychology. Common triggers:
- **FOMO** - "See what top teams are doing differently."
- **Curiosity** - "Want to know how this works?"
- **Reciprocity** - "Get the report, no strings attached."

Strongest ads combine two triggers (e.g., curiosity + social proof, reciprocity + urgency, FOMO + data-backed claims). Don't manipulate - if the problem is real, people will lean in.

## Sender Profile

1. **Send from a founder, CEO, VP, or Director - not SDRs or junior reps.** Trust and credibility matter. Messages come from real people.
   - **Personalization starts with targeting, not copy.** Great copy cannot save bad targeting. Layer: job title + function, seniority, skills/expertise tied to the problem, location (when buying behavior differs by region). If targeting is off, the "personal" illusion breaks instantly.
2. **Use someone the audience can relate to.** If you're targeting marketers, send from a marketer. If you're the founder and you're customer zero, send from yourself. Use sellers only if there's been prior interaction or they're seen as experts.
3. **Profile headline needs to sell.** Bad: "Sales at [Company]." Good: "$XXM+ in pipeline driven through LinkedIn Ads."

## Testing & Benchmarks

- **Open rates:** Aim for 35-50%+
- **CTR:** Expect 2-5%
- **CPC:** $0.30-$0.45
- **Budget:** Start with $2-3K
- **Volume is limited.** You won't send 10K messages in a week unless you have multiple senders.
- **Test subject lines, CTAs, sender profiles, and messaging.** Don't just test delivery - test the copy. Ask: "Would I click this CTA?" and "Does this feel like it's from a real person?"
- **Launch timing must match volume.** Start 2+ weeks before event/deadline with 1 sender. Shorter windows require multiple senders.

## Strategy: TLA + Conversation Ads Combo

A core workflow that works even when you have great creative/video.

1. **Step 1:** Founder/CEO posts on their profile - product releases, industry insights, customer wins, workflows. Simple talking head video on mobile if possible.
2. **Step 2:** Promote those posts with Thought Leader Ads (TLAs) to the target audience. Ensure Campaign Manager is set up correctly - deactivate audience expansion, location auto-expansion, automated bidding.
3. **Step 3:** Retarget people who engaged with the TLAs (or watched 50%+ of video) with Conversation Ads. The convo ad message should relate to the original TLA content.

**Why it works:** Low-effort, quick to launch, uses just copy (no polished creative needed), and the retargeting warm-up means convo ads land with people who already have some familiarity.

## Measurement & Attribution

- **Don't measure conversation ads by last-click alone.** They rarely drive a straight line from click to conversion. They influence interest over time - content views, return visits, assisted conversions across channels.
- **The real value is intent signals.** Every click inside the conversation tells you what the buyer cares about - are they curious, researching, or close to buying? That's more valuable than a single "Submit" button.
- **Downstream actions matter:** website visits, content reads, return visits, assisted conversions - not just form fills. If you only measure clicks, CPL, and last-touch conversions, you're missing most of the story.
- **Open rates are high but don't tell the full story.** Judge by interaction metrics (which options clicked, what content engaged with next, whether they return later).

## High-Performing Message Structure: The Walkthrough Format

Proven structure for demo-oriented conversation ads:

1. **Open with the pain** - 1-2 lines stating the specific problem the audience faces
2. **Name the product as the solution** - 1 line, don't explain everything yet
3. **"In a 20-minute walkthrough, I can show you:"** - frame the demo as a learning session, not a sales call
4. **3 specific bullets** - what they'll see/learn in the walkthrough. Be concrete, not vague.
5. **Personalized close** - "Want to see if it's relevant for %COMPANYNAME%?"
6. **Casual CTAs** - "Yes, let's do it" / "Tell me more first" / "Not interested"

### Why this works:
- Frames the meeting as value exchange, not a sales pitch
- "Walkthrough" feels educational, "demo" feels transactional
- The 3 bullets give the prospect a reason to say yes - they know what they'll get
- %COMPANYNAME% personalization makes it feel targeted, not mass-sent
- Casual CTAs ("Yes, let's do it") outperform formal ones ("Book a demo") because they feel like a text, not a form

### CTA best practices for this format:
- Primary CTA goes to a **Lead Gen Form**, not a scheduling link - reduces friction
- "Tell me more first" branch is where product USPs and social proof live - only shown to people who ask
- Never use "Book a demo" as a CTA label - too sales-y. Use action language: "Yes, let's do it", "Yes, show me", "Let's book it"

### Key principle: USPs belong in the message
Don't just state the problem and say "we handle it." Include at least one specific differentiator in the opening message that makes the prospect think "that's different." The branch messages layer in additional USPs for people who want more detail before committing.

## Common Mistakes to Avoid

- Treating conversation ads like regular ads (they're inbox messages, not feed ads)
- Making them sound like a bot
- Making them too long and spammy
- Failing to test messaging
- Sending from SDRs or generic sales profiles
- Vague messaging - "I'd love to connect" with no clear value
- Using big blocks of text with no paragraph breaks
- Burying CTAs in copy instead of using buttons
- Only measuring clicks/CPL/last-touch - missing downstream influence
- Using buzzwords instead of specific, concrete language
- Generic, overused, or overly sales-driven messaging - buyers are quick to ignore templates
- Expecting instant conversions - conversation ads are for journeys that take time
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/copy-audit-framework.md

```
# Copy Audit Framework

Systematic process for auditing ad copy before it goes live. Applies to all ad formats (document ads, single image, conversation ads, video scripts). Run all 5 layers in order.

## Layer 1: Factual Accuracy

The most critical layer. One wrong claim destroys credibility - especially with technical audiences.

- **Every claim must trace back to verified source material.** If you can't find it in the source posts, product docs, or verified data - don't use it.
- **Regulatory or technical statements must be precise.** "Permitted" vs "required," "expanded" vs "mandated" - these are not interchangeable. Technical audiences will catch errors.
- **Statistics must include source attribution.** A percentage needs its source and sample size - not just the number.
- **Don't add claims not present in verified content.** If the brand never mentioned a specific regulation, framework, or feature in their content - don't add it.
- **Check for implications, not just explicit statements.** "This isn't optional" implies a mandate. "Expanded permissions" implies flexibility. The implication matters as much as the words.
- **When in doubt, use the brand's exact phrasing.** If they frame something a specific way, use their framing - not your interpretation.

### Red flags to check:
- Absolute claims: "required," "mandatory," "the only way," "the biggest"
- Unattributed statistics
- Technical/regulatory framing that contradicts the actual source
- Product capabilities not confirmed in source material
- Claims about competitors or "most companies"

## Layer 2: Tone of Voice

Check against the brand's tone profile - specifically the "What they NEVER do" list first. This catches the biggest violations fastest.

### Check order:
1. **"Never do" list first.** Every brand profile should have one. Questions in body? Urgency tactics? Competitive positioning? Emojis? If any match the "never" list, fix immediately.
2. **Vocabulary.** Are you using their words or yours? Check for: buzzwords they avoid, dramatic language they don't use, qualifiers that don't match their register.
3. **Sentence structure.** Does it match their register? Some brands are punchy (short fragments), others are measured (medium-length, subordinate clauses). Don't impose one on the other.
4. **Headlines.** Informational vs attention-grabbing? Match the brand pattern.
5. **Bridge phrases.** Is the product introduced with the brand's characteristic transition, or does it appear cold? Check the brand file for their pattern.
6. **Point of view.** Neutral/third-person for education, "we/you" for product sections? Match the brand's pattern.
7. **CTAs.** Gentle and specific vs aggressive? Match.

### Common tone violations:
- Using "your" accusatorially ("Your process is broken") when the brand uses third-person observations ("Most teams manage this across 5+ tools")
- Informal slang ("fire drill," "firefighting," "chaos") when the brand is measured
- Marketing pivots ("It doesn't have to be this way") when the brand is educational
- Superlatives ("the biggest," "the best," "the only") when the brand uses measured qualifiers
- Questions in the body when the brand only uses questions in headlines

## Layer 3: Structure & Relevance

Every element must earn its place. No filler, no redundancy.

### For document ads:
- **Does each slide have ONE distinct purpose?** If you can't state the purpose in 5 words, the slide is unfocused.
- **Are adjacent slides redundant?** Common mistake: Slide 5 = "what a good system needs" and Slide 6 = "what our product does" - listing the same capabilities with different words. See `document-ads.md` for the differentiation rule.
- **Does the progression follow the campaign formula?** (Hook -> Pain -> Shift -> Solution -> CTA)
- **Is there a visual break?** Key statistics deserve a standalone slide, not burial in a paragraph.
- **Can any slide be merged with its neighbor without losing information?** If yes, merge.

### For all formats:
- **Is the hook specific enough?** Generic hooks ("Struggling with X?") underperform specific ones that name the real problem.
- **Does the CTA match the funnel stage?** MOF = "See how it works" or "Book a demo." Don't over-ask for BOF actions at MOF stage.
- **Is the post body / introductory text aligned with the creative?** They should feel like the same piece of content.

## Layer 4: Design & Text Density

Ads are visual-first. If a slide or creative looks like a wall of text, people skip it.

- **Would someone swipe past this because it looks dense?** If a slide has 2+ paragraphs plus bullet points, it's too much.
- **Are bullet lists inside content blocks?** Light-colored fills create visual separation and make bullets scannable. Check the brand palette for the right color.
- **Are standalone stats given visual prominence?** See `document-ads.md` for specific sizing guidance.
- **Does every slide have a design note?** The designer needs guidance - don't leave visual treatment to chance.
- **Does the color palette match the brand?** Check every specified color against the brand guide.
- **Is there a visual anchor on product slides?** Screenshot or mockup options give flexibility.

## Layer 5: Audience Fit

The ad must speak the audience's language, not the marketer's.

- **Does the language match how the audience thinks?** Every role has its own frame. Use the audience's terminology and concerns, not generic business language. Check audience insights for specifics.
- **Is the #1 pain point given appropriate prominence?** If audience research identified a primary pain point, it should be central - not one bullet among many.
- **Is the ad specific enough?** Technical audiences respect precision over generalities. Use concrete details the audience recognizes.
- **Are industry terms used where the audience expects them?** Don't simplify jargon for an audience that uses those terms daily.
- **Is the ad different from the other audience variant?** If you have multiple audience versions, each must speak to its audience's specific concerns - not just swap one word.

## Process Summary

| Layer | Primary question | How to check |
|-------|-----------------|--------------|
| 1. Factual Accuracy | Can every claim be verified? | Check every claim against verified source material |
| 2. Tone of Voice | Does it sound like the brand? | Check "never do" list first, then vocabulary/structure |
| 3. Structure & Relevance | Does every element earn its place? | Check each slide/section for distinct purpose |
| 4. Design & Text Density | Would the audience actually read this? | Visual scan for walls of text, check brand palette |
| 5. Audience Fit | Does it speak their language? | Check pain points, terminology, framing vs. audience insights |

Run all 5 layers. Present findings organized by severity: Critical > Significant > Minor. Always include "What Works Well" - the audit should reinforce good decisions, not just flag problems.
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/creative-strategy.md

```
# Creative Strategy for LinkedIn Ads

## Creative by Awareness Stage

Match messaging to where the buyer is in their journey. Each stage requires fundamentally different creative - using the wrong approach wastes budget.

### Problem-Aware

They know they have a pain. They DON'T know what kind of solution can solve it.

**What the buyer is doing:**
- Googling their frustrations ("how to reduce sales no-shows", "too many leads not enough conversion")
- Reading blog posts or social threads about inefficiencies
- Talking to peers about recurring work issues
- Identifying patterns that signal "something is wrong"

**How they want to be approached:**
- Through empathy and validation - "You're not alone"
- With clarity and specificity - show you understand their world
- WITHOUT jargon or product-speak - they're not ready for features
- Stories and specificity - focus on real-world scenarios, pain-led hooks

**What to keep in mind:**
- Highlight the cost of inaction (FOMO, time lost, revenue leakage)
- Tap into emotions: frustration, anxiety, burnout, pressure to hit KPIs
- Avoid naming your product too early - tease the problem behind the problem
- Use punchy metaphors, memes, or workplace lingo to mirror their experience
- Creative should feel like "That's exactly what I'm facing right now"

**Top 5 angles for this stage:**
1. Trigger Event for Buying Journey (regulatory shift, market change, growth pain)
2. Content Promotion (educational content that validates the pain)
3. Main Problem or Challenge Faced (directly name the frustration)
4. Pains with Considered Solutions (why current workarounds don't work)
5. Risk Mitigation - "Avoid the Pain" (consequences of doing nothing)

**Landing page direction:** Problem validation and user empathy, outcome-focused testimonials, introductory brand messaging (NOT feature-led), before/after transformation visuals.

**Format:** TLA (founder voice), educational content, video (10-30s)
**Funnel:** TOF
**CTA:** Learn More

---

### Solution-Aware

They know the solution type exists. They DON'T know which provider is best.

**What the buyer is doing:**
- Comparing vendors on G2, Reddit, "Best X software for Y" blogs
- Asking peers for recommendations
- Evaluating features, pricing, integrations
- Looking for proof: ROI numbers, case studies, product demos
- Watching YouTube reviews or reading analyst reports

**How they want to be approached:**
- With clarity and contrast - they want to know what makes you different
- With real value proof - numbers, case studies, social proof
- With confidence - they're busy and don't want fluff or vagueness
- With efficiency - show how quickly they'll get results

**What to keep in mind:**
- They're already aware of alternatives - don't educate, differentiate
- Product name and category should be present and clear
- Address typical objections and decision-making fears
- Use direct CTAs: "See how we compare," "Why [Company X] switched to us," "Book a demo"
- Competitor angles (light positioning) can work if done smartly

**Top 5 angles for this stage:**
1. Time-to-Value - "See Results Fast" (speed of implementation/ROI)
2. Outcomes - "What You Get" (concrete results, not features)
3. Competitive Advantage - "Be the Leader" (differentiation)
4. Benefits for Users (what changes in their day-to-day)
5. Competitor Comparison - "Why We're Better" (side-by-side, tactful)

**Landing page direction:** Value proposition clarity (headline + benefit + visual), ROI snapshots or short case studies, key differentiators vs competitors, easy getting started paths ("Try free", "Book demo"), optional comparison tables.

**Format:** Video comparisons, document ads, case studies, feature-specific single image
**Funnel:** MOF
**CTA:** Learn More, Read More

---

### Product-Aware

They know you. They DON'T know why they should choose you over alternatives.

**What the buyer is doing:**
- Revisiting your site, case studies, pricing page, or demo flow
- Comparing final options before buying decision
- Looking for reasons to trust you (security, performance, team)
- Reading success stories or niche-specific use cases
- Watching for offers, new features, or product launches

**How they want to be approached:**
- With strong reassurance - "You're not alone, teams like yours use us"
- With social proof - "Join 2,300+ B2B teams using X"
- With precision - highlight exact benefits relevant to their persona/industry
- With urgency or FOMO - "Why wait? Start winning today."

**What to keep in mind:**
- Don't reintroduce the problem - they already know
- Focus on specific results, social validation, and trust-building
- Remove remaining friction: show ease of adoption, support quality, security
- Create urgency through time-based offers or exclusive access
- Personalize if possible - segment by role, use case, or industry

**Top 5 angles for this stage:**
1. Case Studies & Results (specific metrics from real customers)
2. Testimonials (direct quotes, video testimonials)
3. Client Logos (social proof through brand association)
4. Demo-Forward - "Sell the Experience" (show the product, not just talk about it)
5. Key Features and Functionalities (for the final comparison)

**Landing page direction:** Specific benefits per persona/industry, strong social proof (testimonials, results, recognizable logos), product visuals or interactive tours, time-based incentives, final reassurance (support, security, outcomes, guarantees).

**Format:** TLA with CTA, single image with results, Lead Gen Forms, social proof ads (G2, logos)
**Funnel:** BOF
**CTA:** Try Now, Request Demo, Try for Free, Start Free Trial, Book a Demo

---

## 12 Creative Angle Categories

Use these to generate ad ideas. Each angle works at multiple funnel stages but some are stronger at certain stages (noted).

### 1. Trigger Event for Buying Journey
**Rationale:** Address the specific events that prompt buyers to start looking - a new problem, a business environment change, regulatory shift, competitive pressure.
**Why it works:** Demonstrates understanding of the audience's situation and positions the product as timely and necessary.
**Best at:** TOF (Problem-Aware)

### 2. Main Problem or Challenge Faced
**Rationale:** Lead with the primary pain point the audience encounters daily.
**Why it works:** Resonates with lived experience, captures attention with the promise of relief.
**Best at:** TOF (Problem-Aware)

### 3. Goals and Jobs to be Done
**Rationale:** Frame the ad around what the audience is trying to accomplish, not what you sell.
**Why it works:** Positions the product as instrumental in fulfilling their goals and aspirations.
**Best at:** TOF/MOF

### 4. Pains with Considered Solutions
**Rationale:** Highlight the limitations of alternatives or current workarounds.
**Why it works:** Creates contrast, positions your product as the better choice vs the status quo.
**Best at:** MOF (Solution-Aware)

### 5. Key Features and Functionalities
**Rationale:** Educate the audience about what the product actually does and its unique capabilities.
**Why it works:** Provides information for buyers in evaluation mode, demonstrates depth.
**Best at:** MOF/BOF (Solution-Aware, Product-Aware)

### 6. Primary Use Cases
**Rationale:** Show specific real-world scenarios where the product delivers value.
**Why it works:** Makes the solution tangible by illustrating concrete applications.
**Best at:** MOF

### 7. Benefits for Users
**Rationale:** Focus on outcomes, not features. What changes in the user's day-to-day life?
**Why it works:** Appeals to interests and motivations by showcasing positive outcomes.
**Best at:** MOF/BOF

### 8. Selfish Desires Motivating Users
**Rationale:** Tap into underlying personal motivations - career advancement, looking good to the boss, saving time, reducing stress.
**Why it works:** Creates emotional connection beyond business logic, increases engagement.
**Best at:** TOF/MOF

### 9. Case Studies & Results
**Rationale:** Present real customer stories with specific metrics and outcomes.
**Why it works:** Provides social proof and credibility with tangible evidence.
**Best at:** BOF (Product-Aware)

### 10. Testimonials
**Rationale:** Feature direct quotes from satisfied customers or industry experts.
**Why it works:** Builds trust through third-party endorsement and authenticity.
**Best at:** BOF (Product-Aware)

### 11. Ratings and Awards
**Rationale:** Highlight G2, Capterra, Trustpilot ratings, industry awards, or analyst recognition.
**Why it works:** External validation and differentiation from competitors.
**Best at:** BOF (Product-Aware)

### 12. Client Logos
**Rationale:** Display logos of recognizable companies using your product.
**Why it works:** Signals credibility by association - "if they trust it, we can too."
**Best at:** BOF (Product-Aware)

---

## Static Ad Template Categories

Each template type maps to either Prospecting (cold audiences) or Retargeting (warm audiences):

| Category | Type | Best For | Style |
|----------|------|----------|-------|
| Comparison Ad | Prospecting & Retargeting | Differentiation | Before vs After, Expectation vs Reality |
| Text Only Ad | Prospecting | Storytelling | Short narrative, numbers to grab attention |
| Statistics-Based Ad | Prospecting & Retargeting | Authority | Lead with compelling data points |
| Graphs & Diagrams | Prospecting | Education | Explain concepts visually |
| False Interactive Interface | Prospecting | Pattern interrupt | Imitate a functional UI to grab attention |
| Billboard Ad | Retargeting | Brand recall | Imitate real-world objects |
| Meme Ads | Prospecting | Relatability | Humor works in B2B - "Skipping Steps" style |

---

## TLA Content Strategy

TLAs are the #1 format across all sources. Here's how to use them.

**Fundamental truth:** TLAs are just sponsored posts - the same marketing rules that apply to any ad also apply here. Don't assume that because TLAs "feel organic" you can skip the fundamentals of clear messaging, strong CTAs, and audience relevance.

### Why They Win
- Feel organic in the feed (not "ad-like")
- Build personal trust with the founder/executive
- 1.7x higher CTR, 1.6x higher engagement vs standard ads
- CPC as low as $4.14 vs $22+ for brand campaigns
- 3-6x higher CTR than company page ads

### Non-Employee TLAs Outperform Employee TLAs

TLAs from non-employees (industry influencers, thought leaders, creators) consistently outperform TLAs from company employees - typically delivering 15-20% incremental lift in conversions.

**Why it works:** Third-party endorsement feels more credible and objective. When an external thought leader talks about your product, the audience perceives it as genuine recommendation rather than self-promotion.

**How to implement:**
- Partner with industry creators and thought leaders who already have audiences in your ICP
- Have them create content about the problem your product solves (with natural product mention)
- Sponsor their post as a TLA to your target audience

**Budget guidance:** Allocate 30-50% of your TLA budget to non-employee TLAs if you have creator partnerships available.

### TLA Content Best Practices
- Post from the founder or senior executive (not company page)
- Write 1,000-1,500 character posts in problem -> solution -> proof structure
- Run 4-6 variants simultaneously to prevent fatigue
- Rotate every 2-3 weeks
- Content should be genuinely valuable - not just a product pitch
- Address objections head-on with data
- **Connect content to product:** Every TLA should have a dotted line back to what you sell. Pure thought leadership without any product connection is brand building, not demand generation. The connection can be subtle, but it must exist.
- **Create content people would pay for:** The bar for TLA content is "would someone pay money for this insight?" Generic tips and surface-level advice don't work. Personalized demos, in-depth tactical recommendations, and specific frameworks drive engagement.
- **Think awareness, not conversion:** TLAs are fundamentally a brand awareness play. Don't obsess over direct conversions from TLAs. Their value is in building category association and brand recall that manifests later in the buying journey.

### TLA Distribution Tactics

**Organic-first testing:** Use organic posts as a testing ground before promoting as TLAs. Posts that get meaningful DMs and high-quality leads organically are your best candidates for paid promotion. Posts with 2-3% organic CTR tend to be top performers when promoted as ads.

**Post editing trick:** LinkedIn posts get most of their organic reach within ~72 hours. After organic reach dies, edit the post to add a CTA link or product mention, then promote it as a TLA. This way you get the organic engagement from a pure-value post, then convert it to a demand-gen asset for paid.

**Premium CTAs on LinkedIn profile:** LinkedIn Premium allows you to add CTA buttons to your profile (newsletter signup, visit website). Since TLAs drive people to the poster's profile, these CTAs capture additional conversions from profile visitors.

### LinkedIn Profile Optimization for TLAs

Since TLA viewers often click through to the poster's LinkedIn profile, optimize the profile as a landing page:

- **Banner image:** Brand-relevant visual that communicates what you do or sell
- **Profile photo:** Use a brand-color background or border for recognition
- **Headline:** Include your category (not just your title). Example: "CEO at [Company] | Helping B2B teams [outcome]" instead of just "CEO at [Company]"
- **About section:** Include customer testimonials, key results, and a clear explanation of what your company does
- **Featured section:** Pin your core CTA (demo link, top resource) and your best-performing posts

### TLA Metrics Priority

Measure TLA performance in this priority order - most important first:

1. **Demographics report** - Are you reaching the right people? This matters more than any other metric. If demographics are wrong, nothing else matters.
2. **In-platform metrics** - CTR, engagement rate, cost per engagement. These tell you if the content resonates.
3. **Microconversions** - Engaged website visits (time on site, pages per session). These indicate quality traffic.
4. **Self-reported attribution** - "How did you hear about us?" responses that mention LinkedIn or the TLA poster. Captures dark social influence.
5. **Bottom-funnel metrics** - Direct conversions, pipeline, revenue. Important but don't obsess over these for TLAs. Most TLA value is in awareness and consideration, not direct conversion.

### TLA Content Calendar Framework (Monthly)

| Week | Theme | Angle | Format | Funnel |
|------|-------|-------|--------|--------|
| 1-2 | Customer Proof | CSAT scores, real customer quotes, specific results | TLA (Founder) | TOF |
| 1-2 | Pain Point | Address the #1 objection head-on with data | TLA (Founder) | TOF |
| 1-2 | Case Study | Specific customer with specific numbers | Video TLA | TOF/MOF |
| 3-4 | Industry Insight | Thought leadership on the space, not a pitch | TLA (Founder) | TOF |
| 3-4 | Product Demo | Short video showing the product in action | Video | MOF |
| 3-4 | Conversion Push | Direct CTA with customer testimonial | TLA + Single Image | BOF |

---

## Video Ad Guidelines

- TOF: 10-30 seconds (short, hook-focused)
- MOF: 1-2 minutes (demos, deeper case studies)
- Always include subtitles (most LinkedIn users scroll with sound off)
- Hook in first 3 seconds
- End with clear CTA

### Video Strategy for Service Companies

Service companies (agencies, consultancies) that don't have a product demo to show can use this video approach:

- **Cold audience:** Problem/solution videos - address a common pain point and show how your service approach solves it. Keep under 30 seconds, focus on the "aha moment"
- **Remarketing:** Podcast appearance clips - use clips from podcast guest appearances or webinar recordings where your team demonstrates expertise. 60-90 seconds, conversational tone. These feel authentic and build trust through demonstrated knowledge rather than sales messaging

## Creative Rotation Rules

- Refresh any ad running 3+ months
- Maintain 4+ active ads per campaign
- Monitor frequency - above 3x/week per person = time to rotate
- Declining CTR over 2+ consecutive weeks = creative fatigue
- Test different formats: video, document, image, carousel
- For BOF: use social proof formats (G2 ratings, client logos, case studies as static ads)
- New ads: start with even rotation, switch to auto after 7-10 days

## B2B Ad Creative Best Practices

### Address ICP Needs Directly
Use the Jobs-to-be-Done framework: focus on what your buyer is trying to accomplish, not what your product does. Frame ads around the tasks, goals, and outcomes your ICP cares about. Example: "Close deals 40% faster" not "AI-powered CRM with 50+ integrations."

### Copy Rules
- **Direct language:** Use "you" and "your" - speak directly to the reader, not about them
- **Plain speak:** Use the customer's words, not industry jargon. Write at the level your buyer actually talks at, not at conference-speaker level
- **Concise copy:** 60-120 characters for primary text. Every word must earn its place. If a word can be cut without losing meaning, cut it
- **Compelling CTA in headline:** The CTA should be clear and compelling enough to stand alone in the headline. Don't bury it in body copy

### Visual Rules
- **Authentic imagery:** Use real people - team photos, customer photos, behind-the-scenes. No stock photos of smiling businesspeople in glass offices. B2B buyers are trained to ignore stock imagery
- **Brand consistency:** Use consistent colors, fonts, and visual style across all ads so prospects recognize your brand instantly in the feed

### Breaking Norms
- **Mimic in-platform UI elements:** Create ads that look like native LinkedIn interface elements. Example: ads that mimic a LinkedIn notification alert ("John Smith just changed jobs"). This pattern interrupt dramatically increases engagement because it blends with the platform experience
- **Repurpose organic content:** Turn high-performing organic poll results into ad creatives. "87% of our followers said X" is instant social proof

### Lead Gen Form Optimization
- **Message match:** The Lead Gen Form must match the ad message exactly. If the ad promises a "2024 B2B Benchmarks Report," the form title should say exactly that - not a generic "Contact Us"
- **Prefilled fields:** Use LinkedIn's prefilled form fields to reduce friction. Every manual field you add drops completion rate
- **Confirmation message:** Set a clear confirmation message with specific next steps. "Check your email in 5 minutes" is better than "Thanks for submitting"
- **Minimal fields:** Only ask for what you actually need. Name + email + company is sufficient for most TOF/MOF offers. Save detailed qualification for BOF forms

### Social Proof and Trust
- Combine social proof with FOMO: "Join 2,300+ B2B teams" creates both trust and urgency
- Use specific numbers over vague claims: "43% reduction in churn" beats "significant improvement"
- Feature recognizable logos prominently - B2B buyers are risk-averse and follow other companies they respect

## Copy Fundamentals

- Every ad needs a clear CTA
- CTA on ad must match CTA on landing page
- Landing pages must be relevant to ad copy and creative
- No spelling or grammar errors
- Ad sizes suited for the platform - no unintended cropping

## Messaging Foundation

Without product/market fit, ad optimization is nearly impossible. Before investing heavily in ads, ensure:
- Clear ICP definition
- Validated pain points
- Differentiated positioning
- Proof points (customers, data, results)
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/ctv-strategy.md

```
# LinkedIn CTV (Connected TV) Ads

## What It Is

Video ads served on streaming platforms (Roku, Samsung TV, Paramount+, etc.) through LinkedIn's audience targeting. Non-skippable - viewers are forced to watch.

## Key Constraints

- Brand Awareness objective only - no conversion or lead gen objectives available
- Auto-bidding only - no manual bid control
- Minimum $50/day budget
- US and Canada only, English only
- Video specs: 16:9, 4K recommended, 15-30 seconds ideal (under 1 minute max)
- QR code shown at end for tracking

## Why Standard Metrics Don't Apply

- ~100% video completion is the default (forced-view), not a quality signal
- No clicks, no CTR, no engagement rate - none of these exist for CTV
- No direct conversion tracking possible

## How to Measure CTV

- Compare retargeting performance: CTV viewers vs. cold audiences
- Funnel analysis: did CTV-exposed accounts convert faster or at higher rates?
- Self-reported attribution ("How did you hear about us?")
- Data shows: CTV audience 1.6x more likely to visit website, 8% shorter procurement cycles
- CTV + thought leadership retargeting = 3.4x better engagement vs. cold

## When It Makes Sense

- Budgets above $15K/month where brand awareness is a strategic layer
- Paired with a retargeting strategy - CTV warms, other campaigns convert
- When you can measure downstream pipeline impact

## When It Doesn't Make Sense

- Budgets under $15K/month - eats spend from campaigns with measurable signals
- When the account needs direct conversion data to justify spend
- Early-stage accounts that haven't proven their BOF/conversion funnel yet
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/document-ads.md

```
# Document Ads - Best Practices

LinkedIn document ads (carousel format) let users swipe through multiple slides directly in the feed. Strong for MOF nurture - they educate, build trust, and retarget well.

## Format & Specs

- **Slide size:** 1080 x 1350px (portrait 4:5) - ALWAYS. Never 1:1 square for document ads. Square (1080x1080) is for single image ads only.
- **Slide count:** 5-7 for MOF. More than 7 risks drop-off. Fewer than 5 feels thin.
- **File format:** PDF upload (each page = one slide)
- **Can be gated** (Lead Gen Form) or ungated
- **Post body:** Visible above the carousel in the feed. Keep concise - tease the content, don't summarize it.

## 7-Slide Campaign Formula

Tested structure for MOF document ads:

| Slide | Role | Content |
|-------|------|---------|
| 1 | Hook | 3 hook options for A/B testing. Strong headline + subtitle. |
| 2-3 | Pain / cost | What the audience is dealing with. Different per audience segment. |
| 4 | Principle / shift | The "aha" moment. A reframe, a key stat, or a principle that changes how they see the problem. |
| 5-6 | Product solution | What the solution looks like + what the product specifically does. Shared across audiences but framing differs. |
| 7 | CTA | Demo booking. Clean, minimal, brand messaging. |

### Key rule: Slides 5 and 6 must NOT be redundant.

This is the most common structural mistake. Slide 5 describes what any capable system needs (generic requirements). Slide 6 describes what the specific product delivers (unique differentiators). If both slides list the same capabilities with different words, merge or differentiate.

**Good differentiation example:**
- Slide 5: "Multilingual content management - all language versions in one place" (what any solution needs)
- Slide 6: "24 languages with automatic redirection based on user location" (what THIS product uniquely does)

**Bad (redundant):**
- Slide 5: "Multilingual content management"
- Slide 6: "Language management across 24+ markets"

## Copy Principles

### Each slide = one distinct purpose
If you can't state the purpose in 5 words, the slide is unfocused. Write the purpose before writing the copy.

### Use the brand's actual bridge phrases
The transition from education to product is critical. Never introduce the product cold. Every brand has a characteristic way of bridging - check the brand's tone profile for their specific pattern. Without a bridge, the product slide feels like a pitch instead of a logical conclusion.

### CTA slides: declarative, not interrogative
Don't use questions in CTA slides ("If you've ever dealt with...") unless the brand explicitly uses that pattern. Use:
- A clear statement of what the product does
- Recurring brand messaging points the brand already uses
- A specific, gentle CTA matching the brand's CTA style

### Statistics deserve their own slide
A powerful stat buried in a paragraph is wasted. Give it a standalone visual break slide:
- Big number in accent color, large type (120-150pt recommended)
- Minimal surrounding text
- Source attribution in small gray text
- This breaks up text-heavy slides and creates visual rhythm

### Post body conventions
- Tease the content, don't summarize all 7 slides
- Match the brand's formatting conventions (check brand profile for specific symbols/formatting they use)
- Match the tone of the slides - the post body and carousel should feel like one piece

## Design Principles

### Every slide needs a design note
Don't leave visual treatment to chance. Specify:
- Text hierarchy (what's bold, what size, what color)
- Content block fills (use the brand palette for background fills on bullet sections)
- Visual elements (diagrams, screenshots, mockups)
- Whitespace expectations

### Text density kills engagement
Document ads are swiped quickly. If a slide looks like a wall of text, people skip it.
- **Max:** One lead-in sentence + one bullet list per slide, OR one short paragraph + one visual element
- **Never:** Two paragraphs + bullet list on one slide
- **Use content blocks** (light-colored fills from the brand palette) to visually separate bullet lists from surrounding text

### Product slides need visual options
Always provide:
- **Option A:** Real product UI screenshot (if available)
- **Option B:** Designed mockup (browser frame, simplified interface, placeholder data)

This gives flexibility for when the real screenshot isn't available yet.

### Flow diagrams > text descriptions
When showing processes or chains, use a visual diagram with connected nodes. Don't describe a process flow in a paragraph - show it.

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Why it's bad | Fix |
|---------|-------------|-----|
| Adjacent slides that say the same thing | Feels repetitive, wastes a slide | Merge or differentiate (generic requirements vs. specific differentiators) |
| Product introduced without bridge phrase | Feels like a pitch, not a conclusion | Use the brand's characteristic transition phrase |
| Questions in body/CTA when brand doesn't do that | Breaks brand consistency, feels like marketing | Use declarative statements + recurring messaging |
| Unverified claims (especially technical/regulatory) | Destroys credibility with expert audiences | Every claim must trace to verified source material |
| Text-heavy slides with no visual treatment | People swipe past without reading | Content blocks, visual breaks, design notes |
| Dramatic/informal language for measured brands | Audience detects and distrusts it | Use the brand's actual vocabulary |
| Burying key stats in paragraphs | Wastes the most powerful proof point | Standalone visual break slide |
| Same product slide for different audiences | Feels generic, misses audience-specific concerns | Same product, different framing per audience |

## Audience Variant Strategy

When creating the same document ad for multiple audience segments:

| Element | Shared | Different |
|---------|--------|-----------|
| Slide 1 (Hook) | Format, design | Headline, subtitle, pain angle |
| Slides 2-3 (Pain) | - | Fully different - each audience has different daily frustrations |
| Slide 4 (Principle) | Can share the concept | Framing differs per audience's primary concern |
| Slides 5-6 (Product) | Product capabilities are the same | Framing emphasizes what matters most to each audience |
| Slide 7 (CTA) | CTA action (book demo) | Opening statement tailored to audience role |
| Post body | General structure | Pain points and framing tailored |
```

### .claude/skills/linkedin-ads/knowledge-base/full-funnel-framework.md

```
# Full-Funnel LinkedIn Ads Framework
